Title: Are you middle class? It depends who you ask in Congress
Description: <p>The upper bound of "middle class" in America is often pegged at an annual income of between $150,000 and $250,000, but looking at <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/21/mike-johnson-budget-bill-trump-salt-taxes" target="_blank">legislation</a> being drawn up by Republicans in Congress, it seems to be much, much higher.</p><p><strong>Why it matters: </strong>Some of the proposals for the forthcoming budget raise income cutoff levels to as high as $2.5 million per year.</p><hr><p><strong>Driving the news: </strong>The House is considering allowing state and local tax deductions of as much as $40,000 for people making up to $500,000, Axios' <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/21/mike-johnson-salt-deduction-cap-trump-taxes" target="_blank">Hans Nichols</a> reported this week, a sign that some blue-state Republicans consider $500,000 to be a middle-class income.</p><p><strong>Between the lines: </strong>President Trump considers an annual income of $1 million too modest to justify higher <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/09/trumps-millionaires-tax-rates-mike-johnson" target="_blank">income taxes</a>, per Nichols, but is fine with the idea once household income reaches $2.5 million.</p><ul><li>The current incarnation of the bill also raises the level at which the estate tax starts being levied from $15 million to $30 million.</li></ul><p><strong>Where it stands: </strong>To be in <a href="https://www.bls.gov/news.release/wkyeng.t05.htm" target="_blank">the top 10%</a> of individual earners, a U.S. worker has to earn $2,905 per week, per the Bureau of Labor Statistics, which is $150,000 per year.</p><ul><li>To be in <a href="https://dqydj.com/household-income-percentile-calculator/" target="_blank">the top 10%</a> of households in 2024, you had to earn $235,000.</li><li>The <a href="https://www.pewresearch.org/short-reads/2024/09/16/are-you-in-the-american-middle-class/" target="_blank">middle class</a> tops out at $169,800 for a family of three in 2022 dollars, which is $188,400 in 2025 dollars, according to Pew Research Center.</li></ul><p><strong>How it works:</strong> Some government policies only apply to people making less than a certain amount, on the grounds that the rich are already comfortable enough not to need any extra help from Uncle Sam.</p><ul><li>Looking at where that line gets drawn provides an indicator of where the upper middle class ends and the upper class begins, at least in the eyes of Congress.</li></ul><p><strong>Flashback: </strong>Trump, in his first term, sent out stimulus checks to those making $198,000 per year or less for married households filing jointly. People earning more than that amount were considered rich enough not to need the checks.</p><ul><li>Conversely, the net investment <a href="https://www.irs.gov/newsroom/questions-and-answers-on-the-net-investment-income-tax" target="_blank">income tax</a> — a tax on the rich — kicks in only once a married couple filing jointly makes over $250,000 per year.</li><li>For President Biden, $400,000 was the key number, the level <a href="https://bidenwhitehouse.archives.gov/briefing-room/statements-releases/2024/03/11/fact-sheet-the-presidents-budget-cuts-taxes-for-working-families-and-makes-big-corporations-and-the-wealthy-pay-their-fair-share/" target="_blank">below which</a> he said he would never raise taxes, and <a href="https://apnews.com/article/biden-medicare-budget-tax-prescription-drug-costs-99e50b8e956118110dd4a7516318d2cc" target="_blank">above which</a> he wanted a new tax to help pay for Medicare.</li></ul><p><strong>The bottom line: </strong>Now that <a href="https://www.axios.com/local/san-diego/2025/05/16/more-million-dollar-starter-homes-califronia" target="_blank">home values</a> and <a href="https://www.axios.com/2024/08/29/retirement-millionaires-401k-savings" target="_blank">retirement</a> balances regularly make their way into seven-figure territory, we've solidly entered a world of middle-class millionaires.</p>

Title: More Than a Dozen U.S. Officials Sold Stocks Before Trump’s Tariffs Sent the Market Plunging
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/robert-faturechi">Robert Faturechi</a>, <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/pratheek-rebala">Pratheek Rebala</a> and <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/brandon-roberts">Brandon Roberts</a>                </p>
                                             

                                                                    
            
                <p>ProPublica is a nonprofit newsroom that investigates abuses of power. Sign up to receive <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/the-big-story?source=54G&amp;placement=top-note&amp;region=national">our biggest stories</a> as soon as they’re published.</p>

            

                

      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.0">The week before President Donald Trump unveiled bruising new tariffs that sent the <a href="https://www.nbcnews.com/business/markets/us-stocks-dow-nasdaq-sp-plummet-trump-tariffs-rcna199476">stock market plummeting</a>, a key official in the agency that shapes his administration’s trade policy sold off as much as $30,000 of stock. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.1">Two days before that <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/live/2025/04/02/business/trump-tariffs-liberation-day">so-called “Liberation Day”</a> announcement on April 2, a State Department official sold as much as $50,000 in stock, then bought a similar investment as prices fell.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.2">And just before Trump made another significant tariff announcement, a White House lawyer sold shares in nine companies, records show. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.3">More than a dozen high-ranking executive branch officials and congressional aides have made well-timed trades since Trump took office in January, most of them selling stock before the market plunged amid fears that Trump’s tariffs would set off a global trade war, according to a ProPublica review of disclosures across the government.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.4">All of the trades came shortly before a significant government announcement or development that could influence stock prices. Some who sold individual stocks or broader market funds used their earnings to buy investments that are generally less risky, such as bonds or treasuries. Others appear to have kept their money in cash. In one case unrelated to tariffs, records show that a congressional aide bought stock in two mining companies shortly before a key Senate committee approved a bill written by his boss that would help the firms.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.5">Using nonpublic information learned at work to trade securities could violate the law. But even if such actions aren’t influenced by insider knowledge, ethics experts warn that trading stock while the federal government’s actions move markets can create the appearance of impropriety. The recent trades by government officials, they said, underscore that there should be tighter rules on how, or if, federal employees can trade securities.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.6">“The executive branch is routinely engaged in activities that will move the market,” said Tyler Gellasch, who, as a congressional aide, helped write the law on insider trading by government officials and now runs a nonprofit focused on transparency and ethics in capital markets. “I don’t think members of Congress and executive branch officials should be trading securities. To the extent they have investment holdings, it should be managed by someone else outside their purview. The temptation to put their own personal self-interest ahead of their duties to the country is just too high.”</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.0">There is no evidence that the trades by government officials identified by ProPublica were informed by nonpublic information. Still, when government officials trade stock at opportune times, Gellasch said, even if it was based on luck and not inside information, it undermines trust in government and the markets</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.1">“It then becomes a thing where our markets look rigged,” he said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.2">In response to questions from ProPublica, the officials who made the trades either said they had no insider information that would help them time their decisions or did not respond to questions about the transactions. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.3">Questions about trades based on nonpublic information have swirled around Congress for years and began anew after Trump’s tariffs announcements led to wild swings in the market. Lawmakers’ trades are automatically posted online and, after multiple congressional stock-trading scandals, are widely scrutinized as soon as they become public. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.4">But less attention is paid to the trades of executive branch employees and congressional aides whose work could give them access to confidential information likely to influence markets once made public.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.5">Last week, <a href="https://www.propublica.org/article/pam-bondi-trump-media-stock-tariffs">ProPublica reported</a> that Attorney General Pam Bondi <a href="https://extapps2.oge.gov/201/Presiden.nsf/PAS+Index/102E39A4A731A68785258C8A002C7208/$FILE/Pam-Bondi-05.05.2025-278T.pdf">sold between $1 million and $5 million worth</a> of shares of <a href="https://tmtgcorp.com/">Trump Media</a>, the president’s social media company, on April 2. After the market closed that day, Trump unveiled his “Liberation Day” tariffs, sending the market reeling. Bondi’s <a href="https://extapps2.oge.gov/201/Presiden.nsf/PAS+Index/FC3B6F6F496E257B85258C150032F344/$FILE/Bondi,%20Pam%20%20finalEA.pdf">ethics agreement</a> required her to sell by early May, but why she sold on that date is unclear. She has yet to answer questions about the trades, and the Justice Department did not respond to requests for comment. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.6">Earlier this week, <a href="https://www.propublica.org/article/sean-duffy-stock-sales-trump-tariffs">ProPublica reported</a> that Sean Duffy, Trump’s transportation secretary, <a href="https://extapps2.oge.gov/201/Presiden.nsf/PAS+Index/FED6F4F51D33882F85258C8A002C760E/$FILE/Sean-Duffy-04.23.2025-278T.pdf">sold shares in almost three dozen companies</a> on Feb. 11, two days before Trump announced plans to institute wide-ranging “reciprocal” tariffs. A Transportation Department spokesperson said Duffy’s account manager made the trades and that Duffy had no input on the timing.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.7">Using insider government information to buy or sell securities could violate the Stop Trading on Congressional Knowledge, or STOCK, Act. But no cases have ever been brought under the law, and some legal experts have doubts it would hold up to scrutiny from the courts, which in recent years have generally narrowed what constitutes illegal insider trading. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.8">Thousands of government employees are required to file disclosure forms if they sell or buy securities worth more than $1,000. In many cases, the records are available only in person in Washington, D.C., or through a records request. The documents do not include exact amounts bought or sold but instead provide a broad range for the totals of each transaction.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.9">ProPublica examined hundreds of records for trades shortly before major tariff announcements or other key government decisions. Trump, of course, repeatedly said on the campaign trail that he intended to institute dramatic tariffs on foreign imports. But during the first weeks of his term, investors were not panic selling, seeming to assume that his campaign promises were bluster. Several tariff announcements by Trump early on shook the markets, but it wasn’t until he detailed his new tariffs on April 2 that stocks dived. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.10">Among those who sold securities before one of Trump’s main tariff announcements was Tobias Dorsey. Dorsey, a lawyer in the executive branch since the Obama administration, was named acting general counsel for the White House’s Office of Administration in January, when Trump was inaugurated. The division provides a range of services, including research and legal counseling across the president’s staff, including the Office of the United States Trade Representative, which helps craft trade policy. In his LinkedIn bio, Dorsey describes his duties since 2022 as giving “expert advice on a wide range of legal and policy matters to help White House officials achieve their policy goals.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.11">On Feb. 25 and 26, <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950827-tobias-dorsey-03272025-278t/">disclosure records show</a>, Dorsey unloaded shares of an index fund and nine companies, including cleaning products manufacturer Clorox and engineering firm Emerson Electric. The total dollar figure for the sales was between $12,000 and $180,000. (He purchased one stock, defense contractor Palantir, which was selling for a bargain after recently plummeting on news of Pentagon budget cuts.) </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.12">At the time of Dorsey’s trades, investors were still largely in denial that Trump was going to go through with the massive tariffs he had promised during the campaign. But the next morning, Trump posted on social media that significant tariffs on Mexico and Canada “will, indeed, go into effect, as scheduled” in several days, and that “China will likewise be charged an additional 10% Tariff on that date.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.13">The S&amp;P 500, a stock index that tracks a wide swath of the market, fell almost 2% that day alone and ultimately dropped nearly 18% in six weeks.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.14">In an interview, Dorsey said the sale was made by his wife from an account belonging to her. He said she decided to sell around $20,000 worth of shares so they could make tuition payments and that he had no nonpublic information on the impending tariff announcements. The kind of work he does as a career employee, he said, focuses not on public policy, but on how the White House operates, including personnel, workplace technology, contracts and records issues.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.15">“I’m not advising Stephen Miller or Peter Navarro,” he said, referring to top policy advisers to the president. “I’m advising the people running the campus. … I don’t have access to any sensitive political information.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.16">Another well-timed set of transactions was made by Marshall Stallings, the director of intergovernmental affairs and public engagement for Trump’s Trade Representative. The office helps shape the White House’s trade policy and negotiates trade deals with foreign governments.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.17">On March 25 and 27, Stallings sold between $2,000 and $30,000 of stock in retail giant Target and mining company Freeport-McMoRan. The sales appear to have been an abrupt U-turn. He had purchased the shares less than a week earlier. Days after Stallings’ sales, Trump unveiled his most dramatic tariffs. Target stock fell 17%. Freeport-McMoRan fell 25%. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.18">Stallings and the Trade Representative’s office did not respond to multiple requests for comment. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.19">A longtime State Department official, Stephanie Syptak-Ramnath, who until April was ambassador to Peru, also appeared to make a bet against the stock market. On March 24 and 25, she <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950826-stephanie-f-syptak-ramnath-04022025-278t/">sold between $255,000 and $650,000</a> in stocks, and bought between $265,000 and $650,000 in bond and treasury funds (along with $50,000 to $100,000 in stocks). Then, on March 31, two days before Trump’s “Liberation Day” announcement, she sold between $15,000 and $50,000 of a broad-based stock fund. When the market started to plummet, she bought back the same dollar range in another stock fund. Syptak-Ramnath said she did not have any information about the administration's decisions beyond what was publicly available. The trades, she said, were “undertaken as a result of family obligations” and in “response to a changing economy.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.20">A second longtime State Department official, Gautam Rana, who is now ambassador to Slovakia, <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950828-gautam-a-rana-04082025-278t/">sold between $830,000 and $1.7 million</a> worth of stock on March 19, a week before Trump declared new tariffs on cars and two weeks before his “Liberation Day” announcement. The shares he sold were largely broad-based index funds. Rana declined to comment for this story.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.21">Virginia Canter, a former government ethics lawyer, said executive branch employees who don’t have nonpublic information and want to trade stock should consult with ethics officials before doing so, thereby allowing an independent third party to assess their actions.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.22">“If you trade and you don’t seek advice in advance, you kind of do it at your own risk, and if you’re asked about it, you have to hope there aren’t factors that make someone question your motivations,” Canter said. “If you seek ethics official advice, you have some cover.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.23">Executive branch employees are barred from taking government actions that would narrowly benefit them personally, and some are required to sell stock in companies and industries they have purview over in their jobs. But like members of Congress, they are allowed to trade securities. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.24">Since Trump’s tariff announcements and walkbacks began causing fluctuations in the market, questions have been raised about whether anyone has profited off advance notice of the moves. After Trump unexpectedly rolled back some of his tariffs in early April, causing stocks to surge, Rep. Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez warned on social media that “any member of Congress who purchased stocks in the last 48 hours should probably disclose that now.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.25">Rep. Marjorie Taylor Greene bought between $21,000 and $315,000 of stock the day before and the day of the announcement. In a statement, Greene said a financial adviser controls her investments: “Since my portfolio manager makes my trades for me, I usually find out about them when the media asks.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.26">ProPublica’s review of disclosures also found trades by congressional aides that took place before the market tumbled. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.27">Michael Platt, a veteran Republican staffer who served in the Commerce Department during Trump’s first term and now works for the House committee that handles administrative matters for the chamber, restructured his portfolio in <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950851-platt-michael-ptr-2025-04-10/">March</a>. An account under his wife’s name sold off between $96,000 and $390,000 in mostly American companies, and purchased at least $45,000 in foreign stocks and at least $15,000 in an American and Canadian energy index fund. Some stock forecasters considered international markets a relatively safe haven if Trump went through with his tariffs. Platt did not respond to requests for comment.  </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.28">Stephanie Trifone, a Senate Judiciary Committee aide, sold stock in <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950842-trifone-stephanie-ptr-2025-05-12/">mid-March</a> and bought at least $50,000 in treasuries. A spokesperson for the committee’s Democratic minority said Trifone had no nonpublic information about the tariffs and her trades were conducted by a financial adviser without her input. Kevin Wheeler, a staffer for the Senate Appropriations Committee, made a similar move. In late February, he and his spouse <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950844-wheeler-kevin-ptr-2025-05-05/">offloaded between $18,000 and $270,000</a> in funds composed almost entirely of stocks and <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950849-wheeler-kevin-ptr-2025-03-28/">bought between $50,000 and $225,000 in bonds</a>. A spokesperson for the Appropriation Committee’s Republican majority said Wheeler had no nonpublic information about Trump’s tariff plans and that a financial planner made the trades after advising Wheeler to take a more conservative approach with his portfolio.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.29">Another staffer, Ryan White, chief of staff to Sen. James Risch, R-Idaho, <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950840-white-ryan-ptr-2025-04-19/">bought shares worth between $2,000 and $30,000</a> in two precious metals mining companies <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950840-white-ryan-ptr-2025-04-19/">two days</a> before Trump’s “Liberation Day” announcement. He <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950840-white-ryan-ptr-2025-04-19/">continued buying more shares</a> in the companies, Hecla Mining and Coeur Mining, in the following days. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.30">Precious metals can be a safe haven during a bear market turn, but those stocks, like the rest of the market, declined after Trump’s tariff announcements. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.31">Two days after White’s last purchase in April of the mining companies’ shares, however, the firms got some good news. A bill White’s boss introduced to make it easier for mining companies like Hecla and Coeur to operate on public lands was approved by a Senate committee, an important step in passing a bill. (White <a href="https://www.documentcloud.org/documents/25950845-white-ryan-ptr-2025-05-14/">added to his Hecla shares</a> earlier this month and sold his stake in Coeur.)</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.32">White told ProPublica that “all required reporting and ethics rules were followed.” Any suggestion that the committee passing the bill played a role in his stock purchases “is a stretch and patently false,” he said, adding that the legislation “has not become law and even if it does, would take decades to have any appreciable impact.”</p>
        
             

                                    
        
                        <p><strong>Update, May 22, 2025:</strong> This story has been updated to include a statement from Rep. Marjorie Taylor Greene.</p>

        

    
                                         

Title: Help Us Report on Sexual Assault and Misconduct by the Chicago Police Department
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://invisible.institute/dana-brozost-kelleher">Dana Brozost-Kelleher</a>, <a class="name" href="https://invisible.institute/isabelle-senechal">Isabelle Senechal</a> and <a class="name" href="https://invisible.institute/maria-ines-zamudio">María Inés Zamudio</a>, <a href="https://invisible.institute/">Invisible Institute</a>                </p>
                                          

        
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.0">A team of reporters from the nonprofit journalism organizations <a href="https://invisible.institute/introduction">Invisible Institute</a> and <a href="https://www.propublica.org/">ProPublica</a> have reviewed more than 300 sexual assault and misconduct complaints that were filed over the past decade against Chicago police officers.</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.0">But experts say that’s likely an undercount.</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.0"><strong>We need your help to understand the scope and scale of this issue.</strong> We want to talk to people who have experienced sexual misconduct or sexual assault by Chicago police. For this investigation, we defined police sexual misconduct as sexual assault, unwanted, inappropriate touching or comments, and sexual harassment by police officers either on or off duty. Researchers and advocates say sexual misconduct is a “spectrum of behavior.”</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.0">This behavior can include but is not limited to:</p>
            
	<ul>
<li>A Chicago police officer flirting with someone, including by asking for their phone number for reasons not related to a case or by making other inappropriate comments while on duty </li>
<li>A Chicago police officer asking someone for sexual favors in exchange for not ticketing or arresting them</li>
<li>A Chicago police officer sexually assaulting or making unwanted physical contact with someone, including when the officer is off duty</li>
</ul>
</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.0"><a href="https://airtable.com/appx0REbWDR4NzSym/pagFu5m787ZwXmthW/form"><strong>You can share your experience in the form below</strong></a><strong>.</strong> Please also get in touch if you are a current or retired Chicago police officer who has information you can share on this issue.</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.0"><strong>If you prefer to speak with a reporter directly</strong>, you can contact the reporting team by calling or texting 312-488-9552. You can also send a message to reporter María Inés Zamudio on <a href="https://signal.org/download/">Signal</a>, which is more secure, at mizamudio.95.</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.0">We appreciate you sharing your story and we take your privacy seriously. We are gathering this information for the purposes of our reporting, and we will contact you if we wish to publish any part of your story.</p>
            
	<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="9.0">As journalists, our role is to write about issues. We cannot provide legal advice or other support. However, there are resources available. We know these cases can stem from painful experiences, and support is available if you need it:</p>
            
	<ul>
<li>The <a href="https://rainn.org/resources">National Sexual Assault Hotline</a> is available online or by calling 800-656-4673.</li>
<li>The <a href="https://988lifeline.org">National Suicide and Crisis Lifeline</a> is available online or by calling or texting 988.</li>
<li>The Chicago Rape Crisis Hotline can be reached at 888-293-2080.</li>
<li><a href="https://www.ourresilience.org/">Resilience</a><a href="https://www.ourresilience.org/"> in Chicago</a> offers support groups, trauma-informed counseling and advocacy support for survivors.</li>
<li><a href="https://www.caase.org/">Chicago Alliance Against Sexual Exploitation (CAASE)</a> offers legal advice and can make referrals for survivors. </li>
<li><a href="https://the-network.org/">The Network</a> offers support for survivors of domestic violence.</li>
</ul>
</p>
             

				
						
												<p>Andrew Fan, Maheen Khan, Maira Khwaja and Trina Reynolds-Tyler of Invisible Institute contributed reporting, and <a href="https://www.propublica.org/people/ashley-clarke">Ashley Clarke</a> of ProPublica contributed research.</p>

				


      
                               

Title: Chicago Police Dismissed a Recruit’s Claims That a Colleague Sexually Assaulted Her. Then He was Accused Again and Again.
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://invisible.institute/maria-ines-zamudio">María Inés Zamudio</a> and <a class="name" href="https://invisible.institute/dana-brozost-kelleher">Dana Brozost-Kelleher</a>, <a href="https://invisible.institute/">Invisible Institute</a>                </p>
                                             

        
                        <p>This article was produced for ProPublica’s Local Reporting Network in partnership with the <a href="https://invisible.institute/">Invisible Institute</a> and co-published with the <a href="https://chicago.suntimes.com/">Chicago Sun-Times</a> and <a href="https://www.wbez.org/">WBEZ</a>. <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/dispatches">Sign up for Dispatches</a> to get stories like this one as soon as they are published.</p>

        

    
      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.0">Alexus Byrd-Maxey had just finished her second month at the Chicago police academy, well on her way to fulfilling her childhood dream.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.1">The South Side native wanted to become a detective so she could bring closure to families who have lost loved ones to homicides by arresting those responsible.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.2">Byrd-Maxey, then 26, was proud of herself for making it this far: completing college classes, applying to the academy multiple times, passing a background check and physical tests. She was delighted to be part of the academy despite the sacrifices it required, including leaving her toddler son most mornings at 5 a.m. and having her mom spend thousands to buy her new uniforms and equipment.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.3">“Actually sitting in those chairs at the academy was very rewarding,” she said. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.4">But on March 17, 2023, an encounter with a fellow recruit derailed that dream. On that day, she was leaning over a classmate’s computer, helping him log on to do their lesson. As another recruit walked behind her, she said she felt his hands on her waist and his body pressed up against her. He was close enough, she told reporters, that she felt “his penis on my butt.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.5">She recalled that she confronted him immediately — hoping to hear remorse in his voice. Instead, she said, she saw him smirk. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.6">“That’s when I knew,” she said. “You’re not sorry.”</p>
        
    
                    
    
        <p>
            <strong><a href="https://www.propublica.org/getinvolved/sexual-assault-misconduct-chicago-police-callout">Help Us Report on Sexual Assault and Misconduct by the Chicago Police Department</a></strong>
        </p>

    
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.0">In the days that followed, Byrd-Maxey wrestled with how to report her fellow recruit, a man named Eric Tabb, or whether to let it go. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.1">At the same time, police department investigative records show, Tabb started telling other recruits that Byrd-Maxey had overreacted and that he had only tapped her on the shoulder so he could get to his seat. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.2">The following week, when Byrd-Maxey reported the incident to her class leader, he talked to fellow recruits and they downplayed the encounter. A recruit who allegedly witnessed the incident and was friends with Tabb later told investigators that Byrd-Maxey was “trying to victimize herself.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.3">The academy instructor never filed a sexual misconduct complaint. Two and a half weeks later, Byrd-Maxey was fired from the academy for supposedly cursing and using gang language — allegations she has denied. Tabb soon became an officer and began patrolling streets.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.4">But he didn’t last long.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.5">Eight months after Byrd-Maxey was fired, Tabb was arrested for allegedly grabbing a fellow officer’s genitals repeatedly over her uniform after roll call in their police precinct. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.6">That woman, too, had been in the academy with Byrd-Maxey. Two additional female recruits also have said Tabb assaulted them, and witnesses allege yet another was assaulted but did not report it, meaning five of 17 women in his academy class have given similar accounts, investigative files show. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.7">Tabb now faces multiple felony charges, including aggravated criminal sexual abuse. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.8">He has pleaded not guilty, and the criminal case is ongoing. He declined to comment.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.0">Police department records, including interviews and investigative files obtained by the Invisible Institute and ProPublica, together with court records, highlight how police officials failed to intervene while Tabb was at the academy and head off potential harm to other women. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.1">Reporters identified 14 officers who disciplinary records suggest may be repeat offenders, having been accused of sexual assault in the last decade and of at least one other incident of sexual misconduct. Five of those officers faced criminal charges and were convicted, in some cases pleading to a lesser offense that was not a sex crime, and three others have ongoing criminal cases. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.2">In reviewing more than 300 sexual misconduct and assault complaints against Chicago police officers, the Invisible Institute and ProPublica found a pattern of the department failing to vigorously investigate accusations of sexual assault by officers, whether those complaints were lodged by fellow cops or members of the public. The claims were often downplayed or ignored, sometimes allowing officers to abuse again and again.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.3">A 2017 U.S. Department of Justice investigation into Chicago police found officers frequently minimized the seriousness of sexual misconduct accusations against colleagues and didn’t employ best-practice investigative techniques. Police, the investigation found, closed cases without conducting full investigations. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.4">Police spokesperson Don Terry declined requests for an interview but said in a statement that the department “takes all allegations of sexual assault seriously, including allegations against CPD members.” He said the department has “zero tolerance for sexual misconduct and any member in violation will be held accountable.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.5">He also said the department works with victim advocacy groups “to assist in the appropriate reporting of sexual assault allegations against department members in a way that eliminates barriers to reporting and provides support services to the survivors.”</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.0">That Tabb allegedly assaulted women while training to become a police officer is particularly troubling. The police academy is where aspiring officers learn the department’s culture. Recruits get their first lessons in how officers should behave in uniform — and out of it. They also have fewer job protections while undergoing their academy training, so they can be easily dismissed if they break the rules. Four other academy recruits have filed sexual misconduct or harassment complaints in the last four years. Records show three of the complaints ended in no discipline for the accused officer and one is still ongoing. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.1">“I think of it as behavior that you’re sort of taught in the first few years on the job, starting with your field training officer, what you can get away with,” said Philip Stinson, a criminal justice professor at Bowling Green State University who has done extensive research on police misconduct and created a national database of officer arrests. “They learn that it’s accepted behavior within that culture, within that squad, or that shift, quite often. And I do think that it’s behaviors that escalate.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.2">In June 2023, a little over two months after Byrd-Maxey was kicked out of the academy, the department implemented its first sexual misconduct policy, one it had been working on before she began her training.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            Former Chicago police Officer Eric Tabb walks out of a Chicago courthouse after a hearing. He faces multiple charges, including aggravated criminal sexual abuse.
    
            (Jamie Kelter Davis for ProPublica)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
A Pattern of Missed Opportunities
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.0">The Chicago Police Department has a long history of failing to identify and deal with patterns of troubling behavior within its ranks. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.1">Officers who stole from suspects were able to do so repeatedly before getting caught. Detectives who coerced confessions, sending innocent people to prison and costing the city tens of millions of dollars in legal settlements, did so without ever being disciplined. And some cops who abused and tortured Chicagoans did so for years before they were stopped.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.2">This failure of the department to police its own officers has also had devastating consequences for people who have accused officers of sexual assault or harassment. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.3">In 2019, then-Superintendent Eddie Johnson praised the department’s internal investigation of 13-year police veteran Officer Corey Deanes, who had been accused of sexual misconduct by four women. He called it a “testament to our ability to police ourselves.”</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.0">But what Johnson didn’t mention was that the department allowed Deanes to police the city’s streets for nearly a year despite two allegations of sexual misconduct. It took the city’s civilian police oversight agency to identify his behavior and stop him. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.1">Deanes was suspended for seven days in 2011 for sexually harassing a woman during a traffic stop. He insisted on getting her phone number, then called or texted nearly 30 times. He told investigators he had no official reason to pull the woman over but did so because he wanted to talk to her. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.2">Six years later, in August 2017, he again was accused of abusing his position while pulling over a 23-year-old woman during a traffic stop. Deanes, according to internal affairs records, made inappropriate comments to her and threatened to write the woman a ticket if she did not give him her phone number. Then Deanes allegedly hugged her and, though she resisted, touched her buttocks before leaving without giving her a ticket. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.3">The woman reported the incident to the Chicago police and to the Independent Police Review Authority, the civilian oversight agency at the time. Police records and a lawsuit brought by the woman indicate it took police investigators more than a year to interview her, and the department lost an opportunity to get Deanes off the streets. The city settled the woman’s lawsuit for $100,000.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.4">In July 2018, another Chicago woman reported Deanes. She had called 911 for help after arriving home late at night and finding a stranger on her porch. Deanes, who responded to the call, allegedly asked her personal questions, commented on her body and touched her inappropriately, according to internal affairs records.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.5">“I felt so violated,” the woman, who asked to remain anonymous for fear of retaliation, told Invisible Institute and ProPublica. “I called you for help and I got harassed. That is not OK.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.6">The woman reported the incident to the Civilian Office of Police Accountability, the latest iteration of the review board. Launched in 2017 as part of a wider series of reforms, COPA and its new leaders hoped to bring an additional focus to investigating claims of domestic violence and sexual assault committed by officers.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.7">Yet another woman filed a complaint against Deanes two weeks after the incident with the 911 call. This time, COPA identified a pattern. Andrea Kersten, then COPA’s head of investigations, notified the police department’s Bureau of Internal Affairs about the three incidents and pushed for a criminal investigation. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.8">Deanes was arrested in May 2019 and charged with two misdemeanor counts of battery, one felony count of aggravated battery, and three felony counts of official misconduct for the three separate incidents. He pleaded guilty to a reduced charge of misdemeanor battery in 2020 and received two years probation. He was stripped of his Illinois state certification, preventing him from working as a cop.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            A letter from the Chicago Police Department’s Bureau of Internal Affairs outlining the allegations against then-Officer Corey Deanes and suggesting his case be closed after he pleaded guilty to misdemeanor battery in 2020.
    
            (Obtained by Invisible Institute. ProPublica redacted identifying information for the sending, receiving and approving officers; other redactions original.)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="14.0">The woman who encountered Deanes after the 911 call said she’s disappointed that it took so long for the department to take action. “Police will have each other’s backs no matter what heinous things they do,” she said. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="14.1">Deanes declined to comment for this story. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="14.2">COPA has taken steps to improve its handling of sexual misconduct cases to identify officers who repeatedly engage in misconduct. Under Kersten’s leadership, the agency created a Special Victims Squad of trained investigators in 2019 to pursue these cases, including those with limited evidence beyond the accuser’s word. COPA also entered into an agreement that allowed other agencies to conduct joint interviews with victims to enhance collaboration and to reduce the risk of retraumatizing victims by limiting retellings of their assault. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="14.3">Kersten stepped down as the head of COPA in February after facing a possible no-confidence vote from an oversight board and ongoing lawsuits against <a href="https://dockets.justia.com/docket/illinois/ilndce/1:2024cv07376/463117">Kersten and COPA.</a> A lawsuit filed by the Fraternal Order of Police, the union representing Chicago police officers, alleged that COPA and Kersten have exhibited an anti-police bias that unfairly affects investigations and how officers are disciplined. A federal judge dismissed the suit in April.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="16.0">Kersten declined to comment for this story, but in her resignation letter she disputed the claims against her. COPA’s interim chief administrator said the agency remains committed to pursuing investigations of sexual misconduct. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="16.1">“We are working on growing our SVS section to a fuller unit with more staff who will receive specialized training to handle these investigations,” the administrator, LaKenya White, said in a statement. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="16.2">While COPA participated in the investigation into Byrd-Maxey’s claims, internal affairs led the criminal investigation into the matter.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            The Chicago Police Education and Training Academy
    
            (Jamie Kelter Davis for ProPublica)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
“I’m Not Going Nowhere.”
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="18.0">Last fall, Alexus Byrd-Maxey had come to accept what she called God’s will — that she would not become a Chicago police officer. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="18.1">Since being fired from the academy, she had struggled with depression and anxiety as she tried to rebuild her life. She went back to work as a waitress at a North Side restaurant. Yet every time she saw her police uniforms hanging in her closet, she grieved a life she would never have. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="18.2">“I feel like I was stripped away from it,” she said. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="18.3">She thought she was doing the right thing in reporting Tabb, whether he was disciplined or not.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            Byrd-Maxey’s police equipment and uniform from her time as a police recruit
    
            (Jamie Kelter Davis for ProPublica)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="20.0">It wasn’t long after Byrd-Maxey tried to alert the academy about Tabb that a second incident occurred. At the end of August 2023, Tabb attended a birthday celebration at a Wrigleyville bar with other recruits. He joined a female recruit on the dance floor and, according to investigative reports and court records, touched her breast, buttock and crotch over her clothes and also grabbed her face and tried to kiss her. He was so aggressive, a witness told investigators, that a mutual friend had to intervene to get him away from the recruit. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="20.1">A few days later, according to investigative files and court records, Tabb attended a “star party,” an unofficial celebration for graduating recruits receiving their badge number. Another recruit told investigators he saw Tabb grabbing a third female recruit’s genitals over her clothes. The recruit’s boyfriend confronted Tabb, according to a witness. That same night, Tabb touched a fourth recruit’s buttocks, according to interviews with police investigators and court records. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="20.2">In the immediate aftermath, the three women said nothing, and soon Tabb was officially on the force.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="22.0">After about three months, a fifth female officer accused Tabb of touching her crotch over her uniform several times when she stood up after roll call to adjust her duty belt.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="22.1">The woman went to her supervisor despite her fears. “I was afraid that any type of confrontation or anything would not work out for me,” she later told investigators. “I felt powerless,” the woman said, crying. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="22.2">She said Tabb grabbed her crotch a second time.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="22.3">“He sat back in his chair and he had a slight smile on his face with his hand up to his chin,” she said in a recorded interview with investigators. “It looked to me like, I don’t know, like he was proud of what he did.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="22.4">During the investigation into that incident, officials uncovered the allegations against Tabb that involved his off-duty behavior toward recruits. Two of the three recruits stepped forward to report Tabb’s behavior because they said they wanted to support the final alleged victim.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            An internal affairs report outlining the investigation into Tabb’s first alleged assault.
    
            (Obtained by Invisible Institute. ProPublica redacted identifying information for the sending and receiving officers and those who reported and received reports of the incident; other redactions original.)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="24.0">Even though Byrd-Maxey had been the first to report his behavior, no one contacted her for 15 months about the criminal case against Tabb. When she learned about it, she decided to attend his next court hearing. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="24.1">On a Tuesday morning in March, Byrd-Maxey entered the busy Cook County criminal courthouse with her mom, Jauntaunne Byrd-Horne. They walked past the black Doric columns and the tall golden lamps on their way to a courtroom, where a hearing was scheduled in Tabb’s case. For the first time in nearly two years, she was going to see him.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="24.2">Her mom made herself a T-shirt to ensure her daughter’s pain was acknowledged. In bold white letters against a black background, the front of the shirt read: “What about Tabb’s first police academy victim 03/17/2023?”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="24.3">Byrd-Maxey and her mother sat in the courtroom’s first row. A few minutes later, Tabb arrived with his parents and sat behind them. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="24.4">At issue was a request from prosecutors asking to have the additional allegations related to off-duty incidents considered as evidence of a pattern of behavior: similar victims, similar assaults. Dan Herbert, Tabb’s defense attorney, said his client was innocent. He tried to blame Byrd-Maxey for the claims involving all the off-duty recruits, implying she had a grudge against Tabb.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            Byrd-Horne, left, and Byrd-Maxey stand outside the George N. Leighton Criminal Courthouse after a March hearing in the case of former police Officer Eric Tabb.
    
            (Jamie Kelter Davis for ProPublica)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="26.0">Records show that before she was fired, Byrd-Maxey tried to report Tabb multiple times. She talked to her class leader — a fellow recruit who is put in a leadership position — on the Monday following the Friday incident. Records show the class leader discussed the incident with superiors and placed more emphasis on Tabb’s version, which was supported by other recruits. No one filed an official complaint. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="26.1">Almost three weeks later, conflict erupted in class. Byrd-Maxey claimed Tabb verbally attacked her, and Tabb accused her of doing the same, records show. After class, Byrd-Maxey said, she went to her instructor to tell him about the incident in the computer lab. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="26.2">Byrd-Maxey was fired the next day, accused of saying “shut your bitch ass up” in class and using gang-related language. Though their names were redacted, investigative files show seven recruits, including Tabb, filed complaints against Byrd-Maxey for engaging in this behavior. She denies the allegations.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="28.0">The next day, Byrd-Maxey returned to the police academy to file the sexual misconduct complaint herself. She also accused two instructors of wrongful termination. During that investigation, several recruits sided with Tabb, including three who told investigators they were in the computer lab at the time.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="28.1">By the time internal affairs cleared Tabb of Byrd-Maxey’s complaint on Sept. 20, 2023, he had allegedly assaulted three other women from his academy class. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="28.2">During the court hearing, Tabb’s attorney attacked the credibility of those officers for not reporting the alleged abuse the moment it happened. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="28.3">“They’re police officers. They have a duty to report misconduct,” Herbert said. “And they’re probationary police officers, which as the court well knows, they can be fired for any reason, unless it’s an illegal reason. They can be fired for not reporting misconduct. I think that’s what happened in this case.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="28.4">After the hearing, Byrd-Maxey spotted Tabb in the courthouse lobby. She locked eyes with him as their paths crossed.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="30.0">“I’m here. I’m not going nowhere,” she said afterward. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="30.1">Byrd-Maxey and her mother embraced outside the courthouse, an acknowledgment of the toll reporting Tabb had taken on them both. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="30.2">Yet she doesn’t regret it. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="30.3">“I wouldn’t change anything, even though it cost me mentally and financially,” she said. “I wouldn’t change it because, at the end of the day, I had the right motive to be heard and to avoid this from happening again.”</p>


</p>
        
    
                    

    Methodology
<p>Invisible Institute reporters spent over a year identifying allegations of sexual assault and sexual misconduct by Chicago police officers, then created a database from those allegations. We reviewed hundreds of complaints obtained through public records requests and a lawsuit. </p>
<p>Reporters obtained investigative files from the Chicago Police Department’s Bureau of Internal Affairs, the Civilian Office of Police Accountability, and its predecessor agency, the Independent Police Review Authority. Reporters read the original investigative files, examined evidence and reviewed lawsuits, court records and media accounts. </p>
<p>The Invisible Institute and ProPublica compiled a dataset of more than 300 complaints after excluding dozens of cases where body camera footage contradicted the allegation, where non-police witnesses affirmed the police account, or where a secondhand allegation was not corroborated.</p>
<p>This investigation would not be possible without previous litigation that pushed for disciplinary records to be open to the public despite resistance from the city. Those lawsuits include <a href="https://invisible.institute/bond-v-utreras-intervention">Bond v. Utreras</a>, <a href="https://www.chicagotribune.com/2020/07/17/commentary-a-proposed-settlement-would-broadside-police-accountability-that-should-never-happen/">Green v. Chicago Police Department</a> and <a href="https://www.cjr.org/united_states_project/jamie_kalven_diane_bond_chicago_police_misconduct_foia.php">Kalven v. Chicago</a>, which was brought by Jamie Kalven, founder of the Invisible Institute, and led to the publication of police misconduct complaints on the <a href="http://cpdp.co">Civic Police Data Project</a> site.</p>



        
             

                                    
        
                        <p>This story was published with the support of a grant from Columbia University’s Ira A. Lipman Center for Journalism and Civil and Human Rights, in conjunction with Arnold Ventures.</p>

Title: Unlearning or Obfuscating? Jogging the Memory of Unlearned LLMs via Benign Relearning
Description: Machine unlearning is a promising approach to mitigate undesirable memorization of training data in ML models. In this post, we will discuss our work (which appeared at ICLR 2025) demonstrating that existing approaches for unlearning in LLMs are surprisingly susceptible to a simple set of benign relearning attacks: With access to only a small and potentially loosely related set of data, we find that we can “jog” the memory of unlearned models to reverse the effects of unlearning.  For example, we show that relearning on public medical articles can lead an unlearned LLM to output harmful knowledge about bioweapons, and relearning general wiki information about the book series Harry Potter can force the model to output verbatim memorized text. We formalize this unlearning-relearning pipeline, explore the attack across three popular unlearning benchmarks, and discuss future directions and guidelines that result from our study. Our work offers a cautionary tale to the unlearning community—showing that current approximate unlearning methods simply suppress the model outputs and fail to robustly forget target knowledge in the LLMs. What is Machine Unlearning and how can it be attacked? The initial concept of machine unlearning was motivated by GDPR regulations around the &#8220;right to be forgotten&#8221;, [&#8230;]

Title: 特朗普打压哈佛，中国人再次对美国祛魅
Description: 尽管许多象征美国的事物对中国民众的吸引力日益减弱，哈佛等精英大学仍是人们向往或崇拜的对象。特朗普政府针对哈佛的举措可能断绝这种向往，进而重塑两国的整体关系。<p><img src='https://static01.nyt.com/images/2025/05/23/multimedia/23int-china-harvard-01-twjk/23int-china-harvard-01-twjk-articleLarge.jpg' alt='马萨诸塞州坎布里奇的哈佛大学校园，摄于4月。像哈佛这样的美国大学依然对中国学生具有吸引力。为了追求世界一流的教育，中国学生愿意忽视其他顾虑。 Sophie Park for The New York Times'/></p>                    <p style='text-align: right; color: gray; font-size: 11px!important; line-height: 10px;'>Sophie Park for The New York Times</p>                    <p style='text-align: left; color: gray; font-size: 11px!important; line-height: 10px;'>马萨诸塞州坎布里奇的哈佛大学校园，摄于4月。像哈佛这样的美国大学依然对中国学生具有吸引力。为了追求世界一流的教育，中国学生愿意忽视其他顾虑。</p>                    

Title: 特朗普对巴拿马运河的危险痴迷
Description: 特朗普对巴拿马不断施压，这很可能削弱穆里诺政府。一个不稳定的巴拿马对美国毫无益处，点燃这个国家的怒火可能才是最大的风险。<p><img src='https://static01.nyt.com/images/2025/05/22/multimedia/22freeman-03-thwg/22freeman-03-thwg-articleLarge.jpg' alt='巴拿马城。 Rose Marie Cromwell for The New York Times'/></p>                    <p style='text-align: right; color: gray; font-size: 11px!important; line-height: 10px;'>Rose Marie Cromwell for The New York Times</p>                    <p style='text-align: left; color: gray; font-size: 11px!important; line-height: 10px;'>巴拿马城。</p>                    

Title: 特朗普政府称将终止哈佛大学招收国际学生资质
Description: 特朗普为迫使哈佛服从自己议程而采取的施压行动大幅升级。这一举措将直接削弱美国一流大学吸引全世界最优秀、最聪明的学生的能力。哈佛大学可能再次发起法律诉讼。<p><img src='https://static01.nyt.com/images/2025/05/22/multimedia/00dc-harvard-bgwp/00dc-harvard-bgwp-articleLarge.jpg' alt='哈佛校园，摄于本月。美国政府近日正式通知校方，称决定终止其招收国际学生的资质。 Sophie Park for The New York Times'/></p>                    <p style='text-align: right; color: gray; font-size: 11px!important; line-height: 10px;'>Sophie Park for The New York Times</p>                    <p style='text-align: left; color: gray; font-size: 11px!important; line-height: 10px;'>哈佛校园，摄于本月。美国政府近日正式通知校方，称决定终止其招收国际学生的资质。</p>

class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">過去六年，台灣的LGBTQ+社群經歷了最初的狂喜，面對婚姻破裂後的心碎，社會接納上的落差，出櫃與否的糾結，及這一切帶來的挑戰和考驗。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">台灣同婚專法通過的當年即有近3000對同性伴侶登記結婚。根據台灣行政院性別平等處去年發布的最新官方統計，截至2023年底，台灣累計超過2.5萬人（約1萬2千多對伴侶）登記同性婚姻，其中女性近1.8萬人。女性同婚登記人數是男性的2.32倍。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">官方統計也顯示，同志離婚數近年緩步上升，2019年有110對伴侶離婚（2944對結婚），2023年增至783對（3183對結婚），自2019年起至2023年底，共有約2400對同婚伴侶離婚。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-qkj87g e145786u3">台灣同志運動30年：見證同婚專法通過歷程</a><time datetime="2019-05-24" class="e145786u0 bbc-1cg770n e1mklfmt0">2019年5月24日</time></div></li><li dir="ltr" class="bbc-1k00xvf ecaxh2e1"><div data-static-ati-click="https://a1.api.bbc.co.uk/hit.xiti?s=598342&amp;s2=38&amp;p=zhongwen.articles.c628qvee2ppo.trad.page&amp;atc=PUB-[article]-[edoj1]-[]-[CHD=edoj]-[zhongwen.articles.c628qvee2ppo.trad.page]-[]-[]-[]&amp;type=AT" class="bbc-qkj87g e145786u3">台灣同婚專法遺漏的兩岸同性伴侶故事</a><time datetime="2020-05-29" class="e145786u0 bbc-1cg770n e1mklfmt0">2020年5月29日</time></div></li><li dir="ltr" class="bbc-1k00xvf ecaxh2e1"><div class="bbc-1otfsj9 e145786u2"><a href="https://www.bbc.com/zhongwen/trad/chinese-news-55822176" data-static-ati-click="https://a1.api.bbc.co.uk/hit.xiti?s=598342&amp;s2=38&amp;p=zhongwen.articles.c628qvee2ppo.trad.page&amp;atc=PUB-[article]-[edoj1]-[]-[CHD=edoj]-[zhongwen.articles.c628qvee2ppo.trad.page]-[]-[]-[]&amp;type=AT" class="bbc-qkj87g e145786u3">台港跨境同志伴侶：「我們終於可以在台灣結婚」</a><time datetime="2021-01-29" class="e145786u0 bbc-1cg770n e1mklfmt0">2021年1月29日</time></div></li></ul></section><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><h2 id="愛經不起考驗" tabindex="-1" class="bbc-1mewxu4 eglt09e0">愛經不起考驗？</h2></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">對今年40歲的余老師來說，婚姻經營並不容易，同性婚姻尤甚，選擇離婚背後有更多拉扯。除了經濟及生活相處問題，她認為更深層的原因是家庭壓力。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">在台灣同婚合法化前，前妻因擔心被同事認出，拒絕與她在超市同行，「她都說，我先在門口等妳」。但同婚通過後，前妻認為法律認可婚姻，才願意較公開兩人關係。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">然而，婚後兩人租屋在前妻「婆家」不遠處，並共同購買一處預售屋，但前妻為了讓生活「看起來正常」，多數時間選擇住在「婆家」，每週二人世界的相處時間僅剩週末48小時。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">余老師表示，自己是獨生女，父母與親友支持她結婚成家。但前妻來自台灣南投觀念較保守的傳統家庭，母親堅決反對這場婚事。2017年雙方私下舉辦的婚禮，前妻家人僅有一位兄長參與。家庭問題影響婚姻，壓力似乎從未遠離。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">「當時，我懷疑買房子是否只是為了滿足她置產的心願或想像？」余老師說。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">前妻的舉動讓余靜葭自我懷疑：為什麼買了房子仍不夠？之後，加上兩人親密關係的不協調、言語衝突與隔閡加深。這些促使余靜葭在2021年6月提出離婚：「問題太多，我終於無以為繼，」她說，語氣仍帶傷懷與自責。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">余靜葭與前妻在2006年中部求學時相識。一路走來，雖經分合，她心中始終懷抱相守一生的希望。2017年，為結束遠距離戀愛，她搬到農業為主的南投縣，準備與前妻共築未來，並趁同婚運動熱潮，與志同道合的社運團體合作，舉辦民間贊助的婚禮。她提到當年婚禮的喜餅內藏一封聲援同婚合法化的文章，象徵兩人對未來婚姻之路的期許，眼底仍帶一絲懷念往事的笑容。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">「有時我問自己，當初為伴侶來中部，如今留下來為了什麼？」她說。如今，透過性別及教育平權工作幫助小地方的弱勢族群，她一步步找回自我價值。余靜葭未對婚姻失去信心：「婚姻不是束縛，而是為關係提供穩固的框架，」她說。若遇合適的人，她願意再婚。</p></div><figure class="bbc-1qn0xuy"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:74.46%"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/640/cpsprodpb/753b/live/3135f2c0-3235-11f0-8519-3b5a01ebe413.jpg.webp" dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">台灣性別研究學者陳政沂向BBC中文分析說，儘管台灣同志婚姻已合法，「出櫃」仍是許多同志在婚姻前後面臨的高牆。不少同志結婚時，可能僅在戶政系統登記，對外維持「隱形婚姻」，除戶政機關及彼此，無人知曉他們的婚姻。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">「這使得在面對情感衝突時，無法求助親友或專業支持，獨自承受情感與社會的雙重壓力，宛如孤島。」</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">陳政沂解釋，台灣對「離婚」標籤仍存負面觀感，同志離婚身份的揭露常伴隨「婚姻失敗者」與「同志身份」邊緣的雙重污名。因此，許多人為避免負面評價或被深究，離婚後對外人或約會時採取隱晦（discreet）的溝通策略，以「前女友」或「分手」替代前任或離婚。此外，陳先生還觀察到，歐美同志伴侶較少受傳統文化束縛。 相反地，與異性戀伴侶雷同，台灣同志走入婚姻關係前，許多人內化的華人價值觀，例如金錢觀或緊密的家人關係仍會影響婚姻關係及伴侶溝通。他舉例說，有同志便向他表示婚前單身時不大需要或選擇不在意對方的家人，婚後卻需參與對方家庭活動，如過年團聚等互動，開始感到壓力，甚至導致婚姻問題。</p></div><section role="region" aria-label="更多相關內容" data-e2e="scrollable-promos" dir="ltr" class="bbc-1n4f7vm ebmt73l0"><ul dir="ltr" role="list" class="bbc-ekzrm2 ecaxh2e3"><li dir="ltr" class="bbc-1k00xvf ecaxh2e1"><div class="bbc-1otfsj9 class="bbc-qkj87g e145786u3">支持同性婚姻合法化就是「假牧師」？</a><time datetime="2016-12-22" class="e145786u0 bbc-1cg770n e1mklfmt0">2016年12月22日</time></div></li><li dir="ltr" class="bbc-1k00xvf ecaxh2e1"><div class="bbc-qkj87g e145786u3">從陝西到台灣 一個同性戀藝術家的內心掙扎</a><time datetime="2017-09-14" class="e145786u0 bbc-1cg770n e1mklfmt0">2017年9月14日</time></div></li><li dir="ltr" class="bbc-1k00xvf ecaxh2e1"><div class="bbc-1otfsj9 e145786u2"><a href="https://www.bbc.com/zhongwen/trad/world-63850550" data-static-ati-click="https://a1.api.bbc.co.uk/hit.xiti?s=598342&amp;s2=38&amp;p=zhongwen.articles.c628qvee2ppo.trad.page&amp;atc=PUB-[article]-[edoj2]-[]-[CHD=edoj]-[zhongwen.articles.c628qvee2ppo.trad.page]-[]-[]-[]&amp;type=AT" class="bbc-qkj87g e145786u3">新加坡廢除同性性行為禁令 同志團體回憶過往黑暗歷史</a><time datetime="2022-12-05" class="e145786u0 bbc-1cg770n e1mklfmt0">2022年12月5日</time></div></li></ul></section><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><h2 id="「兩個媽媽」家庭的尷尬" tabindex="-1" class="bbc-1mewxu4 eglt09e0">「兩個媽媽」家庭的尷尬</h2></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">對另一位進入同性關係的吳少喬而言，在同婚合法前，面對保守家庭觀念時一敗塗地。現居台灣北部桃園的吳少喬，嘗盡了無法律保障婚姻的苦頭。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">今年43歲的吳老師目前是桃園的兒童性別教育老師。吳少喬2009年與前任同性伴侶在中國舉辦私人婚禮「結婚」。2010年，兩人決定透過試管嬰兒計畫實現生育兒女的夢想。她們從泰國花費45萬新台幣從精子銀行購買精子，原本懷有雙胞胎，但不幸流產一胎。懷孕36週時，吳少喬早產剖腹生下女兒苗苗。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">然而，前妻家人極力反對這段感情，甚至稱吳少喬為「妖女」，揚言要「打斷她的腿」，讓婚姻從一開始便籠罩在烏雲之下。2015年與前妻「離婚」後，吳少喬帶著女兒苗苗回到台灣，決心重建家庭。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">2019年，她與現任妻子明玓再婚，同婚合法化後，現任妻子依法收養苗苗成為繼母。然而，同志家庭仍面臨挑戰。譬如，苗苗就讀中學的表單僅有「父母」欄位，無法反映多元家庭現實。一位校長曾堅持稱吳少喬為「爸爸」，即便她解釋「我們家是兩個媽媽」，校長仍說：「妳有在賺錢，妳就是爸爸！」吳少喬向BBC表示，這顯示台灣教育對多元家庭的準備不足。</p></div><figure class="bbc-1qn0xuy"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:66.57%"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/640/cpsprodpb/773c/live/ca10f5d0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp" srcset="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/240/cpsprodpb/773c/live/ca10f5d0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 240w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/320/cpsprodpb/773c/live/ca10f5d0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 320w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/480/cpsprodpb/773c/live/ca10f5d0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 480w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/624/cpsprodpb/773c/live/ca10f5d0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 624w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/800/cpsprodpb/773c/live/ca10f5d0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 800w" sizes="(min-width: 1008px) 760px, 100vw" alt="吳少喬" loading="lazy" width="1080" height="719" style="aspect-ratio:1080 / 719" class="bbc-139onq"><p class="bbc-1276odk" role="text"><span class="bbc-m04vo2">圖像來源，</span><span lang="en-GB">Provided</span></p></div></figure><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">在華人價值觀的影響下，如同異性戀，也有同志視離婚為恥辱或「不好看的事」，擔憂在親友面前沒面子，因此選擇不對外訴苦。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">陳政沂向BBC說，還有同志離婚者表示，離婚與否也在於對於同志社群的承諾。他說，當年許多人公開結婚曾是驕傲，離婚對一些人來說是「辜負了婚姻平權成果」，譬如有人訴苦稱「我不想讓護家盟（台灣反同婚組織）看笑話！」。 </p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">或者如一些基督徒認為離婚似乎是背叛眾人當年的祝福及信仰而感到痛苦萬分。這些文化與社群期待，加重離婚同志的心理負擔及罪咎感，他說。 </p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">對此，余靜葭則表示，與同為基督徒的前妻曾因性傾向遭教會排斥，離婚後她在南投草屯長老教會找到接納，她哽咽地向記者表示，曾經憂慮離婚而背棄信仰，離婚後的情緒低落及自我懷疑，藉著教會的支持以及協助更弱勢的民眾的成就感，生活逐漸找到方向。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><h2 id="網紅失婚後的自我療傷之路" tabindex="-1" class="bbc-1mewxu4 eglt09e0">網紅失婚後的自我療傷之路</h2></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">35歲的安德烈（Andre）是華人同志圈小有名氣的成人網紅，在2023年在一場親友聚會中接受前夫求婚，恰逢台灣同婚合法化四週年，兩人步入婚姻，獲不少網友祝福。但婚姻僅維持11個月便結束。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">他向BBC表示，離婚後頭三個月陷入情緒低谷，內心充斥被背叛的哀傷：「我花了好長時間調適。」</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">他解釋說，離婚後不僅要向親友解釋原因，外界的評判也讓他倍感壓力，幾近憂鬱。他曾考慮心理諮商，但最終靠自己慢慢調適。為療癒傷痛，安德烈選擇長時間獨處，反思自我與這段關係。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">他考慮過聘請律師處理事業財務分割，但顧及對方家庭經濟狀況，最終放棄訴訟，以和平方式告別婚姻：「從不想見到他，到見面會生氣，再到現在能平靜面對，是一段很長的路。」</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">事實上，台灣同志離婚法律比異性戀夫妻更簡便。依據《同婚專法》，離婚只需具備「難以維持的重大事由」即可申請。反之，台灣《民法》規定，異性戀若過錯由一方負責，僅無責方可請求離婚，這限制「有責配偶」（如外遇者）主動申請裁判離婚，常導致法庭上夫妻互相指責、揭露對方缺失以證明誰是過錯方。直到近年大法官解釋後，情況才有所改善。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">但離婚雖較容易，並不代表每位同志離婚後都能立即無憂無慮的回到單身生活，而不被往事打擾。安德烈向記者說，在社交平台公布離婚後，許多匿名網友冷嘲熱諷湧來。在台灣社群媒體Dcard上，有人針對他和前夫的「開放性關係」批評，認為婚姻應性愛專一，結婚是多此一舉；還有人說早就不看好他們，「這婚姻本就不會長久」。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">安德烈坦言，這些酸言酸語最初傷了他，他也曾試圖向他人解釋選擇，但如今學會釋然：「他們怎麼說是他們的自由，我無法杜絕每個人的悠悠之口。我只能說，每個人對性與關係有不同看法，我與他們三觀不同，但不代表我不能選擇婚姻吧？」</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">走過這段婚姻，安德烈表示目前專注生活與事業，與菲律賓男友維持遠距離戀愛，對再婚持謹慎態度：「未來的事很難說，」他笑著說。</p></div><figure class="bbc-1qn0xuy"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:125.04%"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/640/cpsprodpb/04ee/live/7c7c45e0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp" srcset="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/240/cpsprodpb/04ee/live/7c7c45e0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 240w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/320/cpsprodpb/04ee/live/7c7c45e0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 320w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/480/cpsprodpb/04ee/live/7c7c45e0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 480w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/624/cpsprodpb/04ee/live/7c7c45e0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 624w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/800/cpsprodpb/04ee/live/7c7c45e0-3235-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 800w" sizes="(min-width: 1008px) 760px, 100vw" alt="Andre Chou" loading="lazy" width="1206" height="1508" style="aspect-ratio:1206 / 1508" class="bbc-139onq"><p class="bbc-1276odk" role="text"><span class="bbc-m04vo2">圖像來源，</span><span lang="en-GB">Provided</span></p></div></figure><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><h2 id="「讓同志可以結婚就是要讓同志也可以離婚」" tabindex="-1" class="bbc-1mewxu4 eglt09e0">「讓同志可以結婚，就是要讓同志也可以離婚」</h2></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">台灣心理諮商師徐維庭則向BBC表示，針對多元性別伴侶的諮商資源不足，台灣的政策不夠明確，心理師只能自行宣傳LGBT或多元性別專長。徐維庭建議設立多元性別諮商專區，提供轉介資源，關注多元性別族群的特殊需求。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">台灣人權團體「彩虹平權大平台」執行長鄧筑媛告訴BBC，過去同志伴侶分手時，因缺乏法律規範，常出現暴力或不公情況。「現在有法律協助是很重要的一步，」她說。她解釋說，推動同婚時，律師常說：「讓同志可以結婚，就是要讓同志也可以離婚。」這意味財產分配或子女監護權等「家務事」可進入公領域，依法處理，對同志婚姻至關重要，避免離婚落入無法可管的窘境，儘管許多法令仍不完善。</p></div><figure class="bbc-1qn0xuy"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:64.75%"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/640/cpsprodpb/c9b2/live/cc3cd420-36e3-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp" srcset="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/240/cpsprodpb/c9b2/live/cc3cd420-36e3-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 240w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/320/cpsprodpb/c9b2/live/cc3cd420-36e3-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 320w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/480/cpsprodpb/c9b2/live/cc3cd420-36e3-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 480w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/624/cpsprodpb/c9b2/live/cc3cd420-36e3-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 624w, https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/800/cpsprodpb/c9b2/live/cc3cd420-36e3-11f0-96c3-cf669419a2b0.jpg.webp 800w" sizes="(min-width: 1008px) 760px, 100vw" alt="鄧筑媛" loading="lazy" width="2000" height="1295" style="aspect-ratio:2000 / 1295" class="bbc-139onq"><p class="bbc-1276odk" role="text"><span class="bbc-m04vo2">圖像來源，</span><span lang="en-GB">Provided</span></p></div></figure><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">陳政沂則向BBC表示，根據他的調查，婚姻結束後，男女同志都會尋求支援，但社會給予的資源還遠不夠。此外，多數同志仍對諮商（心理諮詢）都有負面想法，更別提尤其很多同志本來就是在隱形婚姻裡，沒對外說明自己已婚，離婚後更無意願對外求助。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">他說，相較異性離婚者較易在社會中形成支持性網路（譬如，台灣知名電子布告欄PTT便有熱絡的離婚討論區）及許多互助團體及NGO，但離婚同志的聲音在相關社群幾乎缺席，這些因素是影響著需協助的離婚者以及她們的康復路程。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">不過，根據彩虹平權大平台2025年5月發布的電話民調顯示，受訪者中傾向支持同性婚姻仍超過一半，有54.3%的支持者。但專家稱，台灣需要更完善的政策與法律，讓同志伴侶在婚姻或離婚後都能獲得更多有效支持。</p></div><figure class="bbc-1qn0xuy"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:56.25%"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/640/cpsprodpb/1e69/live/9a092330-378a-11f0-8519-3b5a01ebe413.jpg.webp" Title: 特朗普政府叫停哈佛大學招收國際學生，數千在校生亦擔憂受影響
Description: <main role="main" class="bbc-fa0wmp"><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-1151pbn ebmt73l0"><h1 id="content" tabindex="-1" class="article-heading bbc-1tk77pb e1p3vdyi0">特朗普政府叫停哈佛大學招收國際學生，數千在校生亦擔憂受影響</h1></div><figure class="bbc-1qn0xuy"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:56.2%"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/640/cpsprodpb/7088/live/eba84030-377c-11f0-8519-3b5a01ebe413.jpg.webp" Images</span></p></div></figure><section role="region" aria-labelledby="article-byline" class="bbc-1vjaf6b"><strong id="article-byline" aria-hidden="true" class="bbc-m04vo2">Article information</strong><ul role="list" class="bbc-1rvtlej"><li><span role="text"><span class="bbc-m04vo2">Author, </span><span class="bbc-1o0gmgs">凱拉·愛潑斯坦（Kayla Epstein）</span></span></li><li><span role="text"><span class="bbc-m04vo2">Role, <!-- --> </span><span class="bbc-15gut0e">BBC記者</span></span></li><li class="bbc-v8pmqw"><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><time datetime="2025-05-23" class="bbc-xvuncs e1mklfmt0">2025年5月23日凌晨4時01分</time></div></li></ul></section><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">特朗普政府已採取行動，終止哈佛大學招收國際學生的資格，當局與這個美國最古老大學之間的對峙進一步升級。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">美國國土安全部部長克莉絲蒂·諾姆（Kristi Noem）在社群平台X上表示，美國政府已撤銷對哈佛大學「學生與交流訪問學者計劃（SEVP）」的認證，理由是該校「未能遵守法律」。</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">她在週四的貼文中寫道：「這將作為對全國各大學與學術機構的警告。」</p></div><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">哈佛大學則在聲明中稱此舉為「非法行為」。</p></div><section aria-label="廣告 2" aria-hidden="true" role="region" data-e2e="advertisement" class="bbc-geybui"><div id="dotcom-mpu" class="dotcom-ad"></div></section><div dir="ltr" class="bbc-19j92fr ebmt73l0"><p dir="ltr" class="bbc-w2hm1d e17g058b0">哈佛大學回應稱：「我們完全致力於維護哈佛接待國際學生與學者的能力，這些學生和學者來自140多個國家，為哈佛大學乃至整個國家帶來了無可估量的財富。」</p></div><section data-e2e="recommendations-heading" role="region" class="bbc-139onq"></div></div><div class="bbc-6cb6xm"><div class="bbc-1nw71o6"><a href="https://www.bbc.com/zhongwen/articles/cz9yk21vwdno/trad" class="bbc-7tw9s9">丹麥將退休年齡提高至70歲，成為歐洲最高的國家</a></div></div></div></li><li role="listitem"><div data-e2e="recommendations-wrapper" class="bbc-qj66n0"><div class="bbc-i87o2a"><div class="bbc-j1srjl" style="padding-bottom:56.31%;overflow:hidden"><img src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/ace/ws/660/cpsprodpb/976f/live/d8520540-3621-11f0-a597-6d65f2e5ecfb.jpg.webp"
